      <p>
       The
       <b>
        Royal Observatory, Greenwich
       </b>
       (
       <b>
        ROG
       </b>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rmg_1-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rmg-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       known as the
       <b>
        Old Royal Observatory
       </b>
       from 1957 to 1998, when the working
       <b>
        Royal Greenwich Observatory
       </b>
       ,
       <b>
        RGO
       </b>
       , moved from Greenwich to
       <a href="/wiki/Herstmonceux" title="Herstmonceux">
        Herstmonceux
       </a>
       ) is an
       <a href="/wiki/Observatory" title="Observatory">
        observatory
       </a>
       situated on a hill in
       <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Park" title="Greenwich Park">
        Greenwich Park
       </a>
       , overlooking the
       <a href="/wiki/River_Thames" title="River Thames">
        River Thames
       </a>
       . It played a major role in the history of
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">
        astronomy
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Navigation" title="Navigation">
        navigation
       </a>
       , and is best known as the location of the
       <a href="/wiki/Prime_meridian_(Greenwich)" title="Prime meridian (Greenwich)">
        prime meridian
       </a>
       , and thereby gave its name to
       <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Mean_Time" title="Greenwich Mean Time">
        Greenwich Mean Time
       </a>
       . The ROG has the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAU" title="IAU">
        IAU
       </a>
       observatory code of 000, the first in the list.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ROG, the
       <a href="/wiki/National_Maritime_Museum" title="National Maritime Museum">
        National Maritime Museum
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_House" title="Queen's House">
        Queen's House
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Cutty_Sark" title="Cutty Sark">
        Cutty Sark
       </a>
       are collectively designated
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Museums_Greenwich" title="Royal Museums Greenwich">
        Royal Museums Greenwich
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rmg_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rmg-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The observatory was commissioned in 1675 by
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">
        King Charles II
       </a>
       , with the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foundation_stone" title="Foundation stone">
        foundation stone
       </a>
       being laid on 10 August. The site was chosen by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Christopher_Wren" title="Sir Christopher Wren">
        Sir Christopher Wren
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At that time the king also created the position of
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomer_Royal" title="Astronomer Royal">
        Astronomer Royal
       </a>
       , to serve as the director of the observatory and to "apply himself with the most exact care and diligence to the rectifying of the tables of the motions of the heavens, and the places of the fixed stars, so as to find out the so much desired longitude of places for the perfecting of the art of navigation." He appointed
       <a href="/wiki/John_Flamsteed" title="John Flamsteed">
        John Flamsteed
       </a>
       as the first Astronomer Royal. The building was completed in the summer of 1676.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The building was often called "Flamsteed House", in reference to its first occupant.
      </p>
      <p>
       The scientific work of the observatory was relocated elsewhere in stages in the first half of the 20th century, and the Greenwich site is now maintained as a museum.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Observatory,_Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Chronology">
        Chronology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Observatory,_Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Chronology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          This section
          <b>
           does not
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">
            cite
           </a>
           any
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
            sources
           </a>
          </b>
          .
          <span class="hide-when-compact">
           Please help
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Royal_Observatory,_Greenwich&amp;action=edit">
            improve this section
           </a>
           by
           <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">
            adding citations to reliable sources
           </a>
           . Unsourced material may be challenged and
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
            removed
           </a>
           .
          </span>
          <small>
           <i>
            (August 2017)
           </i>
          </small>
          <small class="hide-when-compact">
           <i>
            (
            <a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">
             Learn how and when to remove this template message
            </a>
            )
           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <ul>
       <li>
        1675 – 22 June, Royal Observatory founded.
       </li>
       <li>
        1675 – 10 August, construction began.
       </li>
       <li>
        1714
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Longitude_Act_1714" title="Longitude Act 1714">
         Longitude Act
        </a>
        established the
        <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Longitude" title="Board of Longitude">
         Board of Longitude
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Longitude_rewards" title="Longitude rewards">
         Longitude rewards
        </a>
        . The Astronomer Royal was, until the Board was dissolved in 1828, always an ex officio Commissioner of Longitude.
       </li>
       <li>
        1767 Astronomer Royal
        <a href="/wiki/Nevil_Maskelyne" title="Nevil Maskelyne">
         Nevil Maskelyne
        </a>
        began publication of the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nautical_Almanac" title="Nautical Almanac">
         Nautical Almanac
        </a>
        , based on observations made at the Observatory.
       </li>
       <li>
        1818 Oversight of the Royal Observatory was transferred from the
        <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Ordnance" title="Board of Ordnance">
         Board of Ordnance
        </a>
        to the Board of
        <a href="/wiki/Admiralty" title="Admiralty">
         Admiralty
        </a>
        ; at that time the observatory was charged with maintaining the Royal Navy's
        <a href="/wiki/Marine_chronometer" title="Marine chronometer">
         Marine chronometers
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        1833 Daily time signals began, marked by dropping a
        <a href="/wiki/Time_ball" title="Time ball">
         Time ball
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        1899 The New Physical Observatory (now known as the South Building) was completed.
       </li>
       <li>
        1924 Hourly time signals (
        <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Time_Signal" title="Greenwich Time Signal">
         Greenwich Time Signal
        </a>
        ) from the Royal Observatory were first broadcast on 5 February.
       </li>
       <li>
        1948 Office of the Astronomer Royal was moved to Herstmonceux.
       </li>
       <li>
        1957 Royal Observatory completed its move to Herstmonceux, becoming the Royal Greenwich Observatory (RGO). The Greenwich site is renamed the Old Royal Observatory.
       </li>
       <li>
        1990 RGO moved to Cambridge.
       </li>
       <li>
        1998 RGO closed. Greenwich site is returned to its original name, the Royal Observatory, Greenwich, is made part of the
        <a href="/wiki/National_Maritime_Museum" title="National Maritime Museum">
         National Maritime Museum
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        2011 The Greenwich museums, including the ROG, become collectively the Royal Museums Greenwich.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Royal_Greenwich_Observatory,_Herstmonceux_-_April_2012.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2480" data-file-width="7360" height="74" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Royal_Greenwich_Observatory%2C_Herstmonceux_-_April_2012.jpg/220px-Royal_Greenwich_Observatory%2C_Herstmonceux_-_April_2012.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Royal_Greenwich_Observatory%2C_Herstmonceux_-_April_2012.jpg/330px-Royal_Greenwich_Observatory%2C_Herstmonceux_-_April_2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Royal_Greenwich_Observatory%2C_Herstmonceux_-_April_2012.jpg/440px-Royal_Greenwich_Observatory%2C_Herstmonceux_-_April_2012.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Royal_Greenwich_Observatory,_Herstmonceux_-_April_2012.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Former Royal Greenwich Observatory,
         <a href="/wiki/Herstmonceux" title="Herstmonceux">
          Herstmonceux
         </a>
         , East Sussex
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Site">
        Site
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Observatory,_Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Site">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Illustration_of_Greenwich_Observatory_by_Johann_Doppelmayr.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1d/Illustration_of_Greenwich_Observatory_by_Johann_Doppelmayr.jpg/300px-Illustration_of_Greenwich_Observatory_by_Johann_Doppelmayr.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1d/Illustration_of_Greenwich_Observatory_by_Johann_Doppelmayr.jpg/450px-Illustration_of_Greenwich_Observatory_by_Johann_Doppelmayr.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1d/Illustration_of_Greenwich_Observatory_by_Johann_Doppelmayr.jpg/600px-Illustration_of_Greenwich_Observatory_by_Johann_Doppelmayr.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Illustration_of_Greenwich_Observatory_by_Johann_Doppelmayr.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Greenwich Observatory (Latinized as "Observatorium Anglicanum Hoc Grenovici prope Londinum"), as illustrated in
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johann_Doppelmayr" title="Johann Doppelmayr">
          Doppelmayr
         </a>
         's map of the
         <a href="/wiki/Celestial_sphere" title="Celestial sphere">
          southern celestial hemisphere
         </a>
         , ca. 1730
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       There had been significant buildings on this land since the reign of William I.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenwich_Palace" title="Greenwich Palace">
        Greenwich Palace
       </a>
       , on the site of the present-day Maritime Museum, was the birthplace of both
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_VIII" title="Henry VIII">
        Henry VIII
       </a>
       and his daughters
       <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">
        Mary I
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">
        Elizabeth I
       </a>
       ;
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tudor_dynasty" title="Tudor dynasty">
        the Tudors
       </a>
       used
       <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Castle" title="Greenwich Castle">
        Greenwich Castle
       </a>
       , which stood on the hilltop that the Observatory presently occupies, as a hunting lodge. Greenwich Castle was reportedly a favourite place for Henry VIII to house his mistresses, so that he could easily travel from the Palace to see them.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Establishment">
        Establishment
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Observatory,_Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Establishment">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greenwich_observatory_laser.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="965" data-file-width="945" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Greenwich_observatory_laser.jpg/220px-Greenwich_observatory_laser.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Greenwich_observatory_laser.jpg/330px-Greenwich_observatory_laser.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Greenwich_observatory_laser.jpg/440px-Greenwich_observatory_laser.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greenwich_observatory_laser.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Laser projected from the observatory marking the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prime_Meridian" title="Prime Meridian">
          Prime Meridian
         </a>
         line
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Royal_Observatory.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="979" data-file-width="1280" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Royal_Observatory.jpg/220px-Royal_Observatory.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Royal_Observatory.jpg/330px-Royal_Observatory.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Royal_Observatory.jpg/440px-Royal_Observatory.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Royal_Observatory.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Laser at night
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The establishment of a Royal Observatory was proposed in 1674 by Sir
       <a href="/wiki/Jonas_Moore" title="Jonas Moore">
        Jonas Moore
       </a>
       who, in his role as Surveyor General at the Ordnance Office, persuaded King Charles II to create the observatory, with John Flamsteed installed as its director.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Ordnance Office was given responsibility for building the Observatory, with Moore providing the key instruments and equipment for the observatory at his own personal cost. Flamsteed House, the original part of the Observatory, was designed by Sir
       <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Wren" title="Christopher Wren">
        Christopher Wren
       </a>
       , probably assisted by
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">
        Robert Hooke
       </a>
       , and was the first purpose-built scientific research facility in Britain. It was built for a cost of £520 (£20 over budget) out of largely recycled materials on the foundations of
       <a href="/wiki/Humphrey,_Duke_of_Gloucester" title="Humphrey, Duke of Gloucester">
        Duke Humphrey's Tower
       </a>
       , the forerunner of Greenwich Castle, which resulted in the alignment being 13 degrees away from true North, somewhat to Flamsteed's chagrin.
      </p>
      <p>
       The original observatory at first housed the scientific instruments to be used by Flamsteed in his work on stellar tables, and over time also incorporated additional responsibilities such as marking the official time of day, and housing
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Nautical_Almanac_Office" title="Her Majesty's Nautical Almanac Office">
        Her Majesty's Nautical Almanac Office
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Moore donated two clocks, built by
       <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Tompion" title="Thomas Tompion">
        Thomas Tompion
       </a>
       , which were installed in the 20 foot high Octagon Room, the principal room of the building. They were of unusual design, each with a pendulum 13 feet (3.96 metres) in length mounted above the clock face, giving a period of four seconds and an accuracy, then unparalleled, of seven seconds per day.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Greenwich_Meridian">
        Greenwich Meridian
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Observatory,_Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Greenwich Meridian">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenwich_Meridian" title="Greenwich Meridian">
        Greenwich Meridian
       </a>
      </div>

         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:28-inch_telescope,_Royal_Observatory_and_tree,_Greenwich,_London,_UK,_2015.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="2448" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/28-inch_telescope%2C_Royal_Observatory_and_tree%2C_Greenwich%2C_London%2C_UK%2C_2015.jpg/220px-28-inch_telescope%2C_Royal_Observatory_and_tree%2C_Greenwich%2C_London%2C_UK%2C_2015.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/28-inch_telescope%2C_Royal_Observatory_and_tree%2C_Greenwich%2C_London%2C_UK%2C_2015.jpg/330px-28-inch_telescope%2C_Royal_Observatory_and_tree%2C_Greenwich%2C_London%2C_UK%2C_2015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/28-inch_telescope%2C_Royal_Observatory_and_tree%2C_Greenwich%2C_London%2C_UK%2C_2015.jpg/440px-28-inch_telescope%2C_Royal_Observatory_and_tree%2C_Greenwich%2C_London%2C_UK%2C_2015.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:28-inch_telescope,_Royal_Observatory_and_tree,_Greenwich,_London,_UK,_2015.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Telescope and tree
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Royalobs.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1249" data-file-width="1329" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Royalobs.jpg/220px-Royalobs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Royalobs.jpg/330px-Royalobs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Royalobs.jpg/440px-Royalobs.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Royalobs.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Royal Observatory, Greenwich
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GreenwichChronometer.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="960" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/GreenwichChronometer.jpg/220px-GreenwichChronometer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/GreenwichChronometer.jpg/330px-GreenwichChronometer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/GreenwichChronometer.jpg/440px-GreenwichChronometer.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GreenwichChronometer.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         One of the hyper-accurate timekeepers at the observatory
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       British astronomers have long used the Royal Observatory as a basis for measurement. Four separate meridians have passed through the buildings, defined by successive instruments.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dolan_meridian_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dolan_meridian-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The basis of
       <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">
        longitude
       </a>
       , the meridian that passes through the
       <a href="/wiki/George_Biddell_Airy" title="George Biddell Airy">
        Airy
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Meridian_circle" title="Meridian circle">
        transit circle
       </a>
       , first used in 1851, was adopted as the world's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prime_Meridian" title="Prime Meridian">
        Prime Meridian
       </a>
       at the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Meridian_Conference" title="International Meridian Conference">
        International Meridian Conference
       </a>
       on 22 October 1884 (voting took place on 13 October).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Subsequently, nations across the world used it as their standard for mapping and timekeeping. The Prime Meridian was marked by a brass (later replaced by
       <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">
        stainless steel
       </a>
       ) strip in the Observatory's courtyard once the buildings became a museum in 1960, and, since 16 December 1999, has been marked by a powerful green
       <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">
        laser
       </a>
       shining north across the London night sky.
      </p>
      <p>
       Since the
       <a href="/wiki/Principal_Triangulation_of_Great_Britain" title="Principal Triangulation of Great Britain">
        first triangulation of Great Britain
       </a>
       in the period 1783–1853,
       <a href="/wiki/Ordnance_Survey" title="Ordnance Survey">
        Ordnance Survey
       </a>
       maps have been based on an earlier version of the Greenwich meridian, defined by the transit instrument of
       <a href="/wiki/James_Bradley" title="James Bradley">
        James Bradley
       </a>
       . When the Airy circle (5.79 m to the east) became the reference for the meridian, the difference resulting from the change was considered small enough to be neglected. When a
       <a href="/wiki/Retriangulation_of_Great_Britain" title="Retriangulation of Great Britain">
        new triangulation
       </a>
       was done between 1936 and 1962, scientists determined that in the Ordnance Survey system the longitude of the international Greenwich meridian was not 0° but 0°00'00.417" (about 8 m) East.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Besides the change of the reference line, imperfections of the surveying system added another discrepancy to the definition of the origin, so that the Bradley line itself is now 0°00'00.12" East of the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Ordnance_Survey_Zero_Meridian" title="United Kingdom Ordnance Survey Zero Meridian">
        Ordnance Survey Zero Meridian
       </a>
       (about 2.3m).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       This old astronomical prime meridian has been replaced by a more precise prime meridian. When Greenwich was an active observatory, geographical coordinates were referred to a local
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oblate_spheroid" title="Oblate spheroid">
        oblate spheroid
       </a>
       called a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Datum_(geodesy)" title="Datum (geodesy)">
        datum known as a geoid
       </a>
       , whose surface closely matched local mean sea level. Several datums were in use around the world, all using different spheroids, because mean sea level undulates by as much as 100 metres worldwide. Modern geodetic reference systems, such as the
       <a href="/wiki/World_Geodetic_System" title="World Geodetic System">
        World Geodetic System
       </a>
       and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Terrestrial_Reference_Frame" title="International Terrestrial Reference Frame">
        International Terrestrial Reference Frame
       </a>
       , use a single oblate spheroid, fixed to the Earth's gravitational centre. The shift from several local spheroids to one worldwide spheroid caused all geographical coordinates to shift by many metres, sometimes as much as several hundred metres. The Prime Meridian of these modern reference systems is 102.5 metres east of the Greenwich astronomical meridian represented by the stainless steel strip, which is now 5.31
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcsecond" title="Arcsecond">
        arcseconds
       </a>
       West. The modern location of the Airy Transit is
       <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion">
        <a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Royal_Observatory,_Greenwich&amp;params=51_28_40.1_N_0_0_5.3_W_type:landmark_globe:earth_region:GB_scale:1000&amp;title=Airy+Transit">
         <span class="geo-default">
          <span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">
           <span class="latitude">
            51°28′40.1″N
           </span>
           <span class="longitude">
            0°0′5.3″W
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
         <span class="geo-multi-punct">
          ﻿ / ﻿
         </span>
         <span class="geo-nondefault">
          <span class="vcard">
           <span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">
            51.477806°N 0.001472°W
           </span>
           <span style="display:none">
            ﻿ /
            <span class="geo">
             51.477806; -0.001472
            </span>
           </span>
           <span style="display:none">
            ﻿ (
            <span class="fn org">
             Airy Transit
            </span>
            )
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </a>
       </span>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       International time from the end of the 19th century until
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UT1" title="UT1">
        UT1
       </a>
       was based on
       <a href="/wiki/Simon_Newcomb" title="Simon Newcomb">
        Simon Newcomb
       </a>
       's equations, giving a mean sun about 0.18 seconds behind UT1 (the equivalent of 2.7 arcseconds) as of 2013; it coincided in 2013 with a meridian halfway between Airy's circle and the
       <a href="/wiki/IERS_Reference_Meridian" title="IERS Reference Meridian">
        IERS origin
       </a>
       :
       <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion">
        <a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Royal_Observatory,_Greenwich&amp;params=51_28_40.1247_N_0_0_2.61_W_type:landmark_globe:earth_region:GB_scale:1000">
         <span class="geo-default">
          <span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">
           <span class="latitude">
            51°28′40.1247″N
           </span>
           <span class="longitude">
            0°0′2.61″W
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
         <span class="geo-multi-punct">
          ﻿ / ﻿
         </span>
         <span class="geo-nondefault">
          <span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">
           51.477812417°N 0.0007250°W
          </span>
          <span style="display:none">
           ﻿ /
           <span class="geo">
            51.477812417; -0.0007250
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </a>
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Greenwich_Mean_Time_and_the_time_ball">
        Greenwich Mean Time and the time ball
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Observatory,_Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Greenwich Mean Time and the time ball">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Mean_Time" title="Greenwich Mean Time">
        Greenwich Mean Time
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Greenwich Mean Time (GMT) was until 1954 based on celestial observations made at Greenwich, and later on observations made at other observatories. GMT was formally renamed as
       <a href="/wiki/Universal_Time" title="Universal Time">
        Universal Time
       </a>
       in 1935, but is still commonly referred to as GMT. It is now calculated from observations of extra-galactic radio sources.
      </p>
      <p>
       To help mariners at the port and others in line of sight of the observatory to synchronise their clocks to GMT, Astronomer Royal
       <a href="/wiki/John_Pond" title="John Pond">
        John Pond
       </a>
       installed a very visible
       <a href="/wiki/Time_ball" title="Time ball">
        time ball
       </a>
       that drops precisely at 1 p.m. (13:00) every day atop the observatory in 1833. Initially it was dropped by an operator; from 1852 it was released automatically via an electric impulse from the
       <a href="/wiki/Shepherd_Gate_Clock" title="Shepherd Gate Clock">
        Shepherd Master Clock
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ball-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The ball is still dropped daily at 13:00 (GMT in winter,
       <a href="/wiki/British_Summer_Time" title="British Summer Time">
        BST
       </a>
       in summer).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The ball has several dents sustained during renovations; the builders assumed the historic ball was to be discarded and played football with it.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_14-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ball-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Bomb_attack_of_1894">
        Bomb attack of 1894
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Observatory,_Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Bomb attack of 1894">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Observatory underwent an attempted bombing on 15 February 1894. This was possibly the first "international terrorist" incident in Britain.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The bomb was accidentally detonated while being held by 26-year-old French
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarchist" title="Anarchist">
        anarchist
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Martial_Bourdin" title="Martial Bourdin">
        Martial Bourdin
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Park" title="Greenwich Park">
        Greenwich Park
       </a>
       , near the Observatory building. Bourdin died about 30 minutes later. It is not known why he chose the observatory, or whether the detonation was intended to occur elsewhere. Novelist
       <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Conrad" title="Joseph Conrad">
        Joseph Conrad
       </a>
       used the incident in his novel
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Secret_Agent" title="The Secret Agent">
         The Secret Agent
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_20th_century">
        Early 20th century
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Observatory,_Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Early 20th century">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Britanski_merki_za_dalzhina_Grinuich_2005.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Britanski_merki_za_dalzhina_Grinuich_2005.jpg/220px-Britanski_merki_za_dalzhina_Grinuich_2005.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Britanski_merki_za_dalzhina_Grinuich_2005.jpg/330px-Britanski_merki_za_dalzhina_Grinuich_2005.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Britanski_merki_za_dalzhina_Grinuich_2005.jpg/440px-Britanski_merki_za_dalzhina_Grinuich_2005.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Britanski_merki_za_dalzhina_Grinuich_2005.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Standard lengths on the wall of the Royal Observatory, Greenwich, London – 1 yard (3 feet), 2 feet, 1 foot, 6 inches (1/2 foot), and 3 inches. The separation of the inside faces of the marks is exact at an ambient temperature of 60 °F (16 °C) and a rod of the correct measure, resting on the pins, will fit snugly between them.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
          <a href="#cite_note-17">
           [17]
          </a>
         </sup>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
          <a href="#cite_note-18">
           [18]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       During most of the twentieth century, the Royal Greenwich Observatory was not at Greenwich. The last time that all departments were there was 1924: in that year electrification of the railways affected the readings of the
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">
        Magnetic
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">
        Meteorological
       </a>
       Departments, and the Magnetic Observatory moved to
       <a href="/wiki/Abinger" title="Abinger">
        Abinger
       </a>
       . Prior to this, the observatory had had to insist that the electric trams in the vicinity could not use an earth return for the traction current.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       After the onset of
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
        World War II
       </a>
       in 1939, many departments were temporarily evacuated out of range of German bombers, to Abinger,
       <a href="/wiki/Bradford_on_Avon" title="Bradford on Avon">
        Bradford on Avon
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">
        Bristol
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">
        Bath
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and activities in Greenwich were reduced to the bare minimum.
      </p>
      <p>
       On 15 October 1940, during
       <a href="/wiki/The_Blitz" title="The Blitz">
        the Blitz
       </a>
       , the Courtyard gates were destroyed by a direct bomb hit. The wall above the Gate Clock collapsed, and the clock's dial was damaged. The damage was repaired after the war.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Herstmonceux_Castle">
        Herstmonceux Castle
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Observatory,_Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Herstmonceux Castle">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       After the Second World War, in 1947, the decision was made to move to
       <a href="/wiki/Herstmonceux_Castle" title="Herstmonceux Castle">
        Herstmonceux Castle
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and 320 adjacent acres (1.3 km²), 70 km south-southeast of Greenwich near
       <a href="/wiki/Hailsham" title="Hailsham">
        Hailsham
       </a>
       in East Sussex, due to
       <a href="/wiki/Light_pollution" title="Light pollution">
        light pollution
       </a>
       in London. The Observatory was officially known as The Royal Greenwich Observatory, Herstmonceux. Although the
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomer_Royal" title="Astronomer Royal">
        Astronomer Royal
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Harold_Spencer_Jones" title="Harold Spencer Jones">
        Harold Spencer Jones
       </a>
       moved to the castle in 1948, the scientific staff did not move until the observatory buildings were completed, in 1957. Shortly thereafter, other previously dispersed departments were reintegrated at Herstmonceux.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton_Telescope" title="Isaac Newton Telescope">
        Isaac Newton Telescope
       </a>
       was built at Herstmonceux in 1967, but was moved to
       <a href="/wiki/Roque_de_los_Muchachos_Observatory" title="Roque de los Muchachos Observatory">
        Roque de los Muchachos Observatory
       </a>
       in Spain's
       <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">
        Canary Islands
       </a>
       in 1979. In 1990 the RGO moved again, to
       <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">
        Cambridge
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Following a decision of the
       <a href="/wiki/Particle_Physics_and_Astronomy_Research_Council" title="Particle Physics and Astronomy Research Council">
        Particle Physics and Astronomy Research Council
       </a>
       , it closed in 1998.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Nautical_Almanac_Office" title="Her Majesty's Nautical Almanac Office">
        Her Majesty's Nautical Almanac Office
       </a>
       was transferred to the
       <a href="/wiki/Rutherford_Appleton_Laboratory" title="Rutherford Appleton Laboratory">
        Rutherford Appleton Laboratory
       </a>
       , on the
       <a href="/wiki/Harwell_Science_and_Innovation_Campus" title="Harwell Science and Innovation Campus">
        Harwell Science and Innovation Campus
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Chilton,_Oxfordshire" title="Chilton, Oxfordshire">
        Chilton
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Oxfordshire" title="Oxfordshire">
        Oxfordshire
       </a>
       after the closure. Other work went to the
       <a href="/wiki/UK_Astronomy_Technology_Centre" title="UK Astronomy Technology Centre">
        UK Astronomy Technology Centre
       </a>
       in Edinburgh. The castle grounds became the home of the International Study Centre of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen%27s_University,_Kingston" title="Queen's University, Kingston">
        Queen's University, Kingston, Canada
       </a>
       and The Observatory Science Centre,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which is operated by an educational charity Science Project.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Royal_Greenwich_Observatory_and_Greenwich_Park.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2240" data-file-width="3968" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Royal_Greenwich_Observatory_and_Greenwich_Park.jpg/220px-Royal_Greenwich_Observatory_and_Greenwich_Park.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Royal_Greenwich_Observatory_and_Greenwich_Park.jpg/330px-Royal_Greenwich_Observatory_and_Greenwich_Park.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Royal_Greenwich_Observatory_and_Greenwich_Park.jpg/440px-Royal_Greenwich_Observatory_and_Greenwich_Park.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Royal_Greenwich_Observatory_and_Greenwich_Park.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_House" title="Queen's House">
          Queen's House
         </a>
         at Greenwich, showing the Royal Observatory in the far distance, 2017.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Observatory_museum">
        Observatory museum
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Observatory,_Greenwich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Observatory museum">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The observatory buildings at Greenwich became a museum of astronomical and navigational tools, which is part of the Royal Museums Greenwich.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Notable exhibits include
       <a href="/wiki/John_Harrison" title="John Harrison">
        John Harrison
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Marine_chronometer" title="Marine chronometer">
        sea watch
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/John_Harrison#H4" title="John Harrison">
        the H4
       </a>
       , which received a large reward from the
       <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Longitude" title="Board of Longitude">
        Board of Longitude
       </a>
       , and his three earlier marine timekeepers; all four are the property of the
       <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Defence_(United_Kingdom)" title="Ministry of Defence (United Kingdom)">
        Ministry of Defence
       </a>
       . Many additional horological artefacts are displayed, documenting the history of precision timekeeping for navigational and astronomical purposes, including the mid-20th-century Russian-made F.M. Fedchenko clock (the most accurate pendulum clock ever built in multiple copies). It also houses the astronomical instruments used to make meridian observations and the 28-inch equatorial
       <a href="/wiki/Howard_Grubb" title="Howard Grubb">
        Grubb
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Refracting_telescope" title="Refracting telescope">
        refracting telescope
       </a>
       of 1893, the largest of its kind in the UK. The
       <a href="/wiki/Shepherd_Gate_Clock" title="Shepherd Gate Clock">
        Shepherd Clock
       </a>
       outside the observatory gate is an early example of an
       <a href="/wiki/Clock#Synchronized_or_slave_clocks" title="Clock">
        electric slave clock
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In February 2005 a £16 million redevelopment comprising a new
       <a href="/wiki/Planetarium" title="Planetarium">
        planetarium
       </a>
       and additional display galleries and educational facilities was started; the ROG reopened on 25 May 2007 with the new 120-seat
       <a href="/wiki/Peter_Harrison_Planetarium" title="Peter Harrison Planetarium">
        Peter Harrison Planetarium
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">
        Further reading
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>

      <ul>
       <li>

         Greenwich Observatory: ... the Royal Observatory at Greenwich and Herstmonceux, 1675–1975
        </i>
        . London: Taylor &amp; Francis, 1975 3v. (Vol. 1.
        <i>
         Origins and early history (1675–1835)
        </i>
        , by Eric G. Forbes.
        <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
         ISBN
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-85066-093-9" title="Special:BookSources/0-85066-093-9">
         0-85066-093-9
        </a>
        ; Vol. 2.
        <i>
         Recent history (1836–1975)
        </i>
        , by A.J. Meadows.
        <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
         ISBN
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-85066-094-7" title="Special:BookSources/0-85066-094-7">
         0-85066-094-7
        </a>
        ; Vol. 3.
        <i>
         The buildings and instruments
        </i>
        by Derek Howse.
        <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
         ISBN
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-85066-095-5" title="Special:BookSources/0-85066-095-5">
